  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_c22afe6c-3725-4527-ab80-d7ab400a6ca2'

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer18066519299389936322">One of Beirut’s most celebrated voices, Rabih Alameddine follows his international bestseller, <i>The Hakawati</i>, with a heartrending novel that celebrates the singular life of an obsessive introvert, revealing Beirut’s beauties and horrors along the way.<br/><br/>Aaliya Sohbi lives alone in her Beirut apartment, surrounded by stockpiles of books. Godless, fatherless, divorced, and child</span>
  <span id="freeText18066519299389936322" style="display:none">One of Beirut’s most celebrated voices, Rabih Alameddine follows his international bestseller, <i>The Hakawati</i>, with a heartrending novel that celebrates the singular life of an obsessive introvert, revealing Beirut’s beauties and horrors along the way.<br /><br />Aaliya Sohbi lives alone in her Beirut apartment, surrounded by stockpiles of books. Godless, fatherless, divorced, and childless, Aaliya is her family’s "unnecessary appendage.” Every year, she translates a new favorite book into Arabic, then stows it away. The thirty-seven books that Aaliya has translated have never been read—by anyone. After overhearing her neighbors, "the three witches,” discussing her too-white hair, Aaliya accidentally dyes her hair too blue.<br /><br />In this breathtaking portrait of a reclusive woman’s late-life crisis, readers follow Aaliya’s digressive mind as it ricochets across visions of past and present Beirut. Insightful musings on literature, philosophy, and art are invaded by memories of the Lebanese Civil War and Aaliya’s volatile past. As she tries to overcome her aging body and spontaneous emotional upwellings, Aaliya is faced with an unthinkable disaster that threatens to shatter the little life she has left.<br /><br />A love letter to literature and its power to define who we are, the gifted Rabih Alameddine has given us a nuanced rendering of a single woman's reclusive life in the Middle East.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18066519299389936322" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/2706450-deborah">Deborah</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Yes. The author's employment of Greek mythology into a story of a Lebanese woman, texturized by threads of philosophy, is a comforting yet troubling s<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Yes. The author's employment of Greek mythology into a story of a Lebanese woman, texturized by threads of philosophy, is a comforting yet troubling story. This story of a woman who realizes all too late in life the value of her life, is told beautifully. The vocabulary is complex, challenging...it had me exercising the pages of my Webster's dictionary. In short, I was challenged, I learned and I grew. Isn't that what National Book award winners are supposed to do for a reader? <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/700271-did-aaliyah-s-thoughts-on-israel-in-her">Did Aaliyah&#39;s thoughts on Israel in her recap of Beruit&#39;s wars, pgs 193 - 196 in my copy, affect your feelings about the book? In particular: &quot;Like many nation-states, including its sister pygmy state Lebanon, Israel is an abomination.&quot; She calls her own country an abomination, and noted that she respects many Jews (just not Israelis). I&#39;m not Jewish but I paused at this, and saw it ruined the book for a reviewer.</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_700271'><a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F18114449-an-unnecessary-woman">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/700271-did-aaliyah-s-thoughts-on-israel-in-her">4 years ago</a>

</li>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/46915729-amy"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1586572187i/46915729._UY100_CR16,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/46915729-amy">Amy</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">This is a good question. Infact, some of her remarks were quite positive about Jews but at times she compares Israel and Lebanon equally harshly. She <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">This is a good question. Infact, some of her remarks were quite positive about Jews but at times she compares Israel and Lebanon equally harshly. She also had less positive remarks about other Arab countries and appeared to vacillate on Palestinians (who are kept in refugee camps in Lebanon for decades now) who were eventually some were expelled. The middle east situation is far more complex than the average westerner understands. She also decries the killings by lebaneses on Lebanese, Arab on Arab. I thought she made it clear Beruit's problems were caused by not only many other countries and religios abut the personality of Lebanon itself.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_911994884" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/911994884" />
    <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten"><img alt="Jeffrey Keeten" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1301087352p2/3427339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/911994884?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeffrey Keeten" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten">Jeffrey Keeten</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jeffrey by: </span>
      Nancy
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer911994884" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13929648036658372168"><b>”Although I know the characters of a novel as a collection of scenes as well, as accumulated sentences in my head. I feel I know them better than I do my mother. I fill in the blanks with literary personas better than I do with real people, or maybe I make more of an effort. I know Lolita’s mother better than I do mine, and I must say, I feel her more than I feel my mother. I recognize Rembrandt’s painted face of his mother better than I recognize the real face of mine.”</b><br/><br/><a href='https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Beirut1923_zps2a2671b3.jpg.html' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1551139411i/27118681.jpg' alt=' photo Beirut1923_zps2a2671b3.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><b>Aaliya’s city otherwi</b></span>
  <span id="freeText13929648036658372168" style="display:none"><b>”Although I know the characters of a novel as a collection of scenes as well, as accumulated sentences in my head. I feel I know them better than I do my mother. I fill in the blanks with literary personas better than I do with real people, or maybe I make more of an effort. I know Lolita’s mother better than I do mine, and I must say, I feel her more than I feel my mother. I recognize Rembrandt’s painted face of his mother better than I recognize the real face of mine.”</b><br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Beirut1923_zps2a2671b3.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1551139411i/27118681.jpg" alt=" photo Beirut1923_zps2a2671b3.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>Aaliya’s city otherwise known as Beirut.</b><br><br>Aaliya has issues with her mother. Everytime she looks in the mirror she notices: <i>”I have my mother’s nose, which these days looks like a scimitar buried in slain flesh.”</i> Her mother was always so supportive of her reading...well...not really.. <br><br><i>”Of course I remember various permutations of the ‘Who will want to marry you if you read so much?’ lecture, but I also had to endure the chilly ‘Don’t try to be so different from normal people.’<br><br>Different from normal people? <br><br>When I first hear that, I was sorely offended. I thought every person should live for art, not just me, and furthermore, why would I want to be normal? Why should I want to be stupid like everyone else?”</i><br><br>Aaliya does marry, not a man out of literature although he might have slithered out of a Dicken’s novel as one of his more seedier creations. He certainly isn’t a stride across the moors kind of guy. Fortunately his flag won’t raise and he divorces Aaliya for being barren. <br><br><br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/BookstoreBeirut_zps789e822e.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1551139411i/27118682.jpg" alt=" photo BookstoreBeirut_zps789e822e.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></a><br><b>Beirut Bookstore...Aaliya kept her bookstore much cleaner and more organized than this guy.</b><br><br>Her friend Hannah helps her get a job in a bookstore. It wasn’t easy, the owner a preening fool who wants to own a bookstore just so he can say he owns a bookstore, is looking for a woman with movie star beauty not someone like Aaliya, but one after another these pretty bobbles he hires get married and so he finally, reluctantly, agrees to hire Aaliya. The last thing she is looking for is a husband. The owner, fortunately, hears the siren song of his lavish lifestyle and leaves Aaliya to manage the store however she wishes to. <br><br>He pays her crap wages. <br><br>She makes up for it by stealing books. One by one, they make the furtive journey to her apartment. She has plans for the very best of them. She knows English and French and she begins translating these books into Arabic. <i>”I create and crate.”</i> Once finished she no longer has any interest in them. Publish you say? Perish the thought. Who would be interested in the workings of a madwoman? <br><br>She loves Beirut. <br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/ElizabethTaylor_zps9bc18e6b.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1551139411i/27118683.jpg" alt=" photo ElizabethTaylor_zps9bc18e6b.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></a><br><b>She’s still beautiful, you can’t ignore her, but she is crumbling around the edges. Elizabeth Taylor and Beirut are sisters in spirit.</b><br><br><i>”Beirut is the Elizabeth Taylor of cities: insane, beautiful, tacky, falling apart, aging, and forever drama laden. She’ll also marry any infatuated suitor who promises to make her life more comfortable, no matter how inappropriate he is.”</i><br><br>Of course, like most Americans, what I remember about Beirut happened in 1983 when a suicide truck bomber blew up a Marine barracks killing 241 American servicemen. I didn’t realize until I started reading this book how much resentment I still felt over that incident, even though it wasn’t so much the Lebanese attacking Americans as it was crazy, religious fanatics that happened to be Lebanese. Anyway, it is so cool, that now my somewhat sour view of Beirut has been replaced by this charming, acerbic, book obsessed little old lady. <br><br><b>”When you write about the past, you lie with each letter, with every grapheme, including the goddamn comma.”</b><br><br>If the world was filled with book obsessed readers there would never be civil wars. They are simply too noisy with all the chatter of gunfire and the screaming of wounded people, not to mention the thump of mortars exploding may cause books to leap from their shelves. It is difficult with the constant barrages of noises to settle down properly with a good book. <br><br>It just wouldn’t do. <br><br>So who is Aaliya’s favorite philosopher? <br><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<i>The philosopher I feel the most kinship with is Spinoza; I identify with his story and his life. The Jewish elders of Amsterdam issued a cherem--a fatwa, for you non-Hebrew speakers--against my kinsman when he was a mere twenty-three. He was excommunicated for his heresies. He didn’t fight it, didn’t rebel. He didn’t even whine. He gave up his family inheritance and became a private scholar; a philosopher at home.”</i> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Spinoza_zps865ac910.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1551139411i/27118680._SY540_.jpg" alt=" photo Spinoza_zps865ac910.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></a><br><b>The Philosopher</b><br><br>So what does Aaliya think about Faulkner vs. Hemingway?<br><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<i> “I consider it a shame that most contemporary American writing seems informed more by Hemingway, the hero of adolescent boys of all ages and genders, than by the sui generis genius of letters, Faulkner. A phalanx of books about boredom in the Midwest is lauded (where the Midwest lies is a source of constant puzzlement to me, somewhere near Iowa I presume), as are books about unexplored angst in New Jersey or couples unable to communicate in Connecticut.</i> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> It was Camus who asserted that American novelists are the only one who think they need not be intellectuals.<br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/FaulknervsHemingway_zps816550e1.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1551139411i/27118679.jpg" alt=" photo FaulknervsHemingway_zps816550e1.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></a><br><br>She digresses in the middle of her story with such gems as this.<br><br><i>”In one of his essays, (Javier) Marias suggest that his work deals as much with what didn’t happen as with what happened. In other words, most of us believe we are who we are because of the decisions we’ve made, because of events that shaped us, because of the choices of those around us. We rarely consider that we’re also formed by the decisions we didn’t make, by events that could have happened but didn’t, or by our lack of choices, for that matter.”</i><br><br>I’ve had one of those weeks where words like this are so much more profound; and though scary, are after more pondering...comforting. <br><br>There are so much more that I wish I could share with you, but I want every person who reads this review to read this book. You have to know how this mild mannered woman acquires an AK-47. It makes me smile every time I think about it. You need to make a list of all the fabulous books that with just a few lines of praise from Aaliya will have you salivating to read them. You must read about her Joseph Conradesque mishap. Her bottle blue misfortune. <br><br>You will have to wait until nearly the last page before she reveals what her favorite book is. It is an interesting choice. <br><br><b>”When I read a book, I try my best, not always successfully, to let the wall crumble just a bit, the barricade that separates me from the book. I try to be involved. <br><br>I am Rasholnikov. I am K. I am Humbert and Lolita.<br><br>I am you."</b><br><br>I do believe I’m in love with a 72 year old Lebanese woman living in Beirut. <br><br>If you wish to see more of my most recent book and movie reviews, visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.jeffreykeeten.com">http://www.jeffreykeeten.com</a><br>I also have a Facebook blogger page at: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten">https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten</a></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="13929648036658372168" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_827466349" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/827466349" />
    <a title="Elyse  Walters" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/597461-elyse-walters"><img alt="Elyse  Walters" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514246949p2/597461.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/827466349?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elyse  Walters" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elyse  Walters" href="/user/show/597461-elyse-walters">Elyse  Walters</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer827466349" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3549182017279127023">Update... $1.99 kindle special. Fabulous book at a great price - in case you missed it! <br/>I took one small issue with the book<br/>- but already had enough discussions about it for a lifetime. Lol<br/>But .... really a VERY enjoyable book!! <br/><br/><br/><br/>4.5 Rating! Until I came to page 195 --I was sure I was going to give this book a 5 star rating. <br/><br/>The positives for this book are STRONG!!!!! The negative-- on the top half of page 195 does not sit right with me! <br/><br/>This is what The New York Times wrote about this book: ....</span>
  <span id="freeText3549182017279127023" style="display:none">Update... $1.99 kindle special. Fabulous book at a great price - in case you missed it! <br />I took one small issue with the book<br />- but already had enough discussions about it for a lifetime. Lol<br />But .... really a VERY enjoyable book!! <br /><br /><br /><br />4.5 Rating! Until I came to page 195 --I was sure I was going to give this book a 5 star rating. <br /><br />The positives for this book are STRONG!!!!! The negative-- on the top half of page 195 does not sit right with me! <br /><br />This is what The New York Times wrote about this book: ....(I 'almost' agree)<br />"An Unnecessary Woman" is a meditation on, among other things, aging, politics, literature, loneliness, grief, and resilience. If there are flaws to this beautiful and absorbing novel, they are not readily apparent". <br /><br />YES...'THE FLAW' --(on page 195) --completely shifted my 'zen-space-of-enjoyable-reading-experience'! --What the author wrote at the top of page 195 did not ADD ANY value to the storytelling --(it wasn't worth the risk)...<br />Being Jewish-- I was offended! I would bet Israeli's might feel pissed! <br />But damn --I don't want to make this entire review about one paragraph! Yet ---make clear --I didn't like it! <br /><br />As for the rest of the book (personal emotions aside for page 195)...<br /><br />I LOVED LOVED 'EVERYTHING' else!!!! The 72 year old narrator -grabs your attention with the first sentence of the book --(and keeps it -with every word she writes -to its end)<br /><br />The first sentence: <br />...."You could say I was thinking of other things when I shampooed my hair blue, and two glasses of red wine didn't help my concentration". <br /><br />Other Quotes which stand out for me:<br />...."I long ago abandoned myself to a blind lust for the written word. Literature is my sandbox. In it I play, build my forts and castles, spend glorious time. It is the world outside that box that gives me trouble. I have adapted tamely, though not conventionally, to this visible world so I can retreat without much inconvenience into my inner world of books. Transmuting this sandy metaphor, if literature is my sandbox, then the real world is my hourglass -- an hourglass that drains grain by grain. Literature gives me life, and life kills me. <br />Well, life kills everyone." <br /><br />....In one of the few Hemingway stories that I don't find wholly insufferable, "Hills Like White Elephants", ...... <br /><br />I'm interested in something the author wrote...(the narrator's voice), about her take on Hemingway and American writers. <br />I found it fascinating what the narrator thought about American contemporary writers. She said, "its only American writers that don't seem to think they need to be intellectuals". A bold statement. (maybe a little arrogant, to boot)<br /><br />...."Pessoa, more a connoisseur of alienation than even Flaubert, wrote: "I've surrounded the garden of my being with high iron gratings --more imposing than any stone wall-- in such a way that I can perfectly see others while perfectly excluding them, keeping them in their place as others". <br /><br />Wonderful book --I loved the literature parts the best. Several authors I don't know: Bruno Schulz, Federico Garcia Lorca --to name a couple. (yet, I plan to look them up)...<br /><br />Whew...well, this review turned out longer than I should be asking any of you to read....<br />Please forgive me!<br /><br />Its a phenomenal book... (yes, I felt angry with the narrators 'opinion'...having a raw gut reaction: its just too personal for 'me'), otherwise, this novel is beautiful! Worth reading!</span>
  <a data-text-id="3549182017279127023" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_815777148" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/815777148" />
    <a title="Tony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33771-tony"><img alt="Tony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1292869016p2/33771.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/815777148?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tony" href="/user/show/33771-tony">Tony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=top-10-2014">top-10-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=autographed">autographed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=lebanese">lebanese</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer815777148" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14236585417670914720">Rabih Alameddine is a name-dropper. By page 61 of this really exceptional novel he had dropped Sebald, Bolano, Svevo, Pessoa, Javier Marías, Dickens, Calvino, Balzac, Nabokov, Conrad, Donne, Bataille, Miller, Moravia, Shulz, Chekov, de Sade, Jong, Keats, Proulx, Garner, Rilke, Marquez, Burroughs, Mann, Becket, Welty, Saramago, Cioran and his favorite Arab writers of erotica: al-Tifashi, al-Tijani and al-Tusi. He has something to say about each of them. And then, in a flourish, in the next two pa</span>
  <span id="freeText14236585417670914720" style="display:none">Rabih Alameddine is a name-dropper. By page 61 of this really exceptional novel he had dropped Sebald, Bolano, Svevo, Pessoa, Javier Marías, Dickens, Calvino, Balzac, Nabokov, Conrad, Donne, Bataille, Miller, Moravia, Shulz, Chekov, de Sade, Jong, Keats, Proulx, Garner, Rilke, Marquez, Burroughs, Mann, Becket, Welty, Saramago, Cioran and his favorite Arab writers of erotica: al-Tifashi, al-Tijani and al-Tusi. He has something to say about each of them. And then, in a flourish, in the next two pages, he rattles off Camus, Duras, Faulkner, Hemingway, Díaz, Hemon, Coetzee, Gordimer, Farah, Malouf, Kundera, Kadare, Tolstoy, Gogol, Hamsun, Borges, Nooteboom, Karasu, Nádas, Kiš, and Kafka. I may have missed a few; and he doesn’t stop there. There will be much to say about Proust.<br /><br />Alameddine is able to do this without being as entirely gratuitous as Hollywood nudity by making his female protagonist, Aaliya Saleh, a bookstore employee, a thief of books from that same bookstore, and a solitary, secret translator of great literature.<br /><br />Aaliya is 72. After a very brief marriage, her impotent husband, <i>a listless mosquito with a malfunctioning proboscis</i>, in compliance with Lebanese law, stood before her in their living room and announced, “Woman, you are divorced.” <i>Tfeh</i>, Aaliya remembers. She is cynical and crusty, and happy to be alone with her books. (Although, she has less good to say about McEwan and Hemingway than she does about humanity in general.)<br /><br />I keep saying <i>she</i>, but it is of course really Alameddine speaking through her. This is a novel for bibliophiles, a Smörgåsbord of literary allusions, opinions, even reviews. But it is also a poem to the discovery of music, one that almost precisely mirrored my own. Aaliya would see a reference to a musical piece in her reading and then seek out the music itself, first as vinyl but later on CD. With limited funds, she wondered what artist, what composer, what orchestra to buy. She liked the yellow label of <i>Deutsche Grammophon</i>, and figured Germans would know how to produce the loftiest of music, so that informed her selections. It would be Pogorelich’s <i>Chopin</i> she would hear. It was a feeling of déjà vu as I read this, remembering a college immersion into Hesse, and how that led me to the library to find <i>St. Matthew’s Passion</i>, and how, even now, the rows of yellow labels stare back at me, including Pogorelich’s <i>Chopin</i>.<br /><br />But this book was not simply Alameddine showing off his shelves. To speak of Love, he had Aaliya’s friend write in her diary that “My heart had momentarily found its pestle.” And he writes with humor:<br /><br /><i>You can tell how well a marriage is working by the number of bite marks on each partner’s tongue. Mr. Hayek had none. </i><br /><br />Of the city of his birth, where the events of the novel take place: <i>Beirut is the Elizabeth Taylor of cities: insane, beautiful, tacky, falling apart, aging, and forever drama laden. She’ll also marry any infatuated suitor who promises to make her life more comfortable, no matter how inappropriate he is.</i><br /><br />Alameddine quotes Alain Robbe-Grillet to say that the worst thing to happen to the novel was the arrival of psychology. <i>If this were a novel, you would be able to figure out why my mother screamed.</i> Hah! <i>I’ve read so many recent novels, particularly those published in the Anglo world, that are dull and trite because I’m always supposed to infer causality.</i> Whoa! Who you calling ‘Anglo’? But, seriously, he really put his finger on something there, didn’t he? <br /> <br />This was intelligent, controlled, poignant. I read on the edge of my seat, not looking for what would happen next, but for what he would <i>say</i> next. Like this:<br /><br /><i>I may be able to explain the difference between baroque and rococo, between South American magical realism and its counterparts in South Asia and sub-Saharan Africa, between Camus’s nihilism and Sartre’s existentialism, between modernism and its post, but don’t ask me to tell you the difference between the Nasserites and the Baathists. …<br /><br />Samir Kassir … differentiates them thus: Arab nationalists who converted to socialism and socialists newly alert to the mobilizing virtues of nationalism.<br /><br />Decipher that.<br /><br />Need I tell you that Baathists and Nasserites have killed each other by the busload?<br /><br />One’s first response is that these Beirutis must be savagely insane to murder each other for such trivial divergences. Don’t judge us too harshly. At the heart of most antagonisms are irreconcilable similarities. Hundred-year wars were fought over whether Jesus was human in divine form or divine in human form. Belief is murderous.</i><br /><br />Aaliya: a life lived amongst music and books. Epicurean. <i>Non fui; fui; non sum; non curo.</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14236585417670914720" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_847344194" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/847344194" />
    <a title="Sue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3642045-sue"><img alt="Sue" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274829293p2/3642045.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/847344194?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sue" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sue" href="/user/show/3642045-sue">Sue</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18975644-an-unnecessary-woman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=lebanon">lebanon</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=favorite-authors">favorite-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=net-galley">net-galley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=plan-to-re-read">plan-to-re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=re-read-2017">re-read-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=my-own-books">my-own-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer847344194" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1680834188298552517">Update to review, October, 2017:<br/><br/>First, I agree with my initial review completely. I love the book, again, in the same and new ways. This time I read it more slowly, giving attention to Alameddine's prose, his style and the actual words and phrases he used in describing Aaliya, her neighbors, family, neighborhood, and city. This time I became captivated by Aaliya in a different way--by her struggle with an uncaring family in her youth, a struggle that has lasted her entire life; by the strength o</span>
  <span id="freeText1680834188298552517" style="display:none">Update to review, October, 2017:<br /><br />First, I agree with my initial review completely. I love the book, again, in the same and new ways. This time I read it more slowly, giving attention to Alameddine's prose, his style and the actual words and phrases he used in describing Aaliya, her neighbors, family, neighborhood, and city. This time I became captivated by Aaliya in a different way--by her struggle with an uncaring family in her youth, a struggle that has lasted her entire life; by the strength of her will and her intellect in the face of a society who has little room for her; by her struggle with an aging body in a war-ravaged city trying to renew itself; by her struggle to find purpose in her life of books, a life she has kept hidden from the world.<br /><br />What I found on this second reading were signs of the world's encroachment into her solitude, in tiny ways to be sure, but in ways that didn't cause her to run, that led her to wonder about tomorrow and to wonder who might be IN her tomorrow besides herself. So I am quietly even more happy at the end of this reading than I was when I finished three+ years ago. Thank you Mr. Alameddine.<br /><br />...........................................................................................................................................................<br /><br />Absolutely wonderful on so many levels. <br />An Unnecessary Woman will undoubtedly be high on my list of favorite books of 2014. I've already planned to read it again and many of the books that Aaliya, our narrator, mentions, are on my reading list already. Some have now jumped higher! When I read about this book, I learned that it was about a woman who worked in a bookstore and translates books into Arabic. Oh but it is so much more than this! It is the story of Beirut and Aaliya, the personal and religious and political and civic wars they have been through together. It is the story of a woman who has found a way to survive and how books and the life of the mind are both solace and salvation---except when they aren't.<br /><br />I have so many highlighted sections that it becomes difficult to choose. Shall I pick one related to her family, her home, her city, her books? Aaliya says:<br /><br /><br /> <i>I can dig out the old chestnut from George Santayana, <br /> that "those who cannot remember the past are condemned to<br /> repeat it," but it serves no purpose. It's a hopelessly<br /> optimistic quote. We are condemned to repeat the past<br /> whether we remember it or not. It is inevitable; just ask <br /> Nietzsche (eternal return) or Hegel (history repeats itself)<br /> or James McCourt (history repeats itself like hiccups)....<br /> I'm fond of Mark Twain's quote: "History doesn't repeat <br /> itself, but it does rhyme."</i><br /> (loc 2164)<br /><br /><br />As you can see from the end of that selection there is humor amidst the gloom. There are some very funny moments and some extremely touching ones that made me remember moments with loved ones. I hesitate to share too many. I want the reader to experience them for themselves.<br /><br />It will not matter if you do not know all the books Aaliya mentions though it did add a spark to my reading to know that I would be reading some soon. Another surprise for me (and it probably shouldn't be) is that the author is male. He captures this aging woman so well.<br /><br />Well, enough said. I could write forever but I must end. Read this book.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br />A copy of this book was provided by the publisher through NetGalley in return for an honest review.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1680834188298552517" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_985003036" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/985003036" />
    <a title="Richard Derus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus"><img alt="Richard Derus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494992210p2/3920693.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/985003036?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Derus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Derus" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus">Richard Derus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3920693-richard-derus?shelf=gifted">gifted</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer985003036" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11636964083998764859">Rating: 4.25* of five<br/><br/><b>The Publisher Says</b>: One of Beirut’s most celebrated voices, Rabih Alameddine follows his international bestseller, <i>The Hakawati</i>, with a heartrending novel that celebrates the singular life of an obsessive introvert, revealing Beirut’s beauties and horrors along the way.<br/><br/>Aaliya Sohbi lives alone in her Beirut apartment, surrounded by stockpiles of books. Godless, fatherless, divorced, and childless, Aaliya is her family’s "unnecessary appendage.” Every year, she translates a n</span>
  <span id="freeText11636964083998764859" style="display:none">Rating: 4.25* of five<br><br><b>The Publisher Says</b>: One of Beirut’s most celebrated voices, Rabih Alameddine follows his international bestseller, <i>The Hakawati</i>, with a heartrending novel that celebrates the singular life of an obsessive introvert, revealing Beirut’s beauties and horrors along the way.<br><br>Aaliya Sohbi lives alone in her Beirut apartment, surrounded by stockpiles of books. Godless, fatherless, divorced, and childless, Aaliya is her family’s "unnecessary appendage.” Every year, she translates a new favorite book into Arabic, then stows it away. The thirty-seven books that Aaliya has translated have never been read—by anyone. After overhearing her neighbors, "the three witches,” discussing her too-white hair, Aaliya accidentally dyes her hair too blue.<br><br>In this breathtaking portrait of a reclusive woman’s late-life crisis, readers follow Aaliya’s digressive mind as it ricochets across visions of past and present Beirut. Insightful musings on literature, philosophy, and art are invaded by memories of the Lebanese Civil War and Aaliya’s volatile past. As she tries to overcome her aging body and spontaneous emotional upwellings, Aaliya is faced with an unthinkable disaster that threatens to shatter the little life she has left.<br><br>A love letter to literature and its power to define who we are, the gifted Rabih Alameddine has given us a nuanced rendering of a single woman's reclusive life in the Middle East.<br><br><b>My Review</b>: The Doubleday UK meme, <a href="http://expendablemudge.blogspot.com/2014/07/a-july-organizational-game.html" rel="nofollow noopener">a book a day for July 2014</a>, is the goad I'm using to get through my snit-based unwritten reviews. Today's prompt is to discuss your favorite novel in translation. So far this year, this is my hands-down favorite novel translated from the furrin. **CORRECTION** The novel was written in English, but it's so beautiful I don't want to take it down!<br><br><br>What does it mean to be invisible? If you choose not to interact with the world, become a recluse, divest yourself of close relationships and divorce yourself from the life of the boudoir, and seal yourself away in a capsule formed of books and words, you are a freak. Aaliya's neighbors think she's a ruined woman. Aaliya's customers at the bookstore she works at, intellectuals all, don't notice her enough to form an opinion, and her family (absent the dearest companion of her life, her *true* family, a departed friend) hasn't given her much attention at all.<br><br>She lives in Beirut, that once-fabulous once-gorgeous ruin on the Mediterranean, an early victim of the endless idiotic religious wars of the region. Aaliya represents Beirut's decline from a world-class cultural center to a shuttered mass of broken buildings holding wary, angry people.<br><br>Aaliya is an angry woman, or at least I see her as such, and has walled herself in to avoid the nasty consequences of being angry amid armed and angry men. She would not be isolated if Beirut wasn't what it is, I think, because she is a reflection of the energy of that wounded and dying place. She preserves her sanity by translating her beloved books, the beauties of which she renders into the sinuous sonorous rhythms of Arabic. And then, like she does with her self, she packages them up and puts them away. They are safe. They are invisible.<br><br>This is tragic. This is a sin. A woman, a mere woman, cannot be her full self; a book, a useless object, cannot spread its beauties for fear that it will not be appreciated or will be used as a weapon by the religious idiots.<br><br>And this is the reason I give this book over four stars. Alameddine has created a literary person's most deeply felt example of why the world appears to be headed directly for the bottom of the septic tank: Aaliya reads and thinks on and renders the majesty and magic of words into the language of her people, and then cannot, will not, dare not allow them out of her keeping.<br><br>This book should have made me feel claustrophobic. It appears to be a scream from within the coffin that anti-intellectual religious idiots are all but nailing shut around the world. (Creation SCIENCE?! REALLY?!) Instead I felt...uplifted in a curious way, heartened, encouraged. Alameddine sees it too! He created this most marginal of marginal beings, the unmarried childless woman intellectual in an Islamic society, and set her to singing. Aaliya sings her thoughts, sings her translations, warbles her precious quotes to herself, her best and only audience. She makes beauty from beauty as she sits and rots in the cesspool of gawd.<br><br>I don't know if this is a cautionary tale, an elegy, or the quietest jeremiad of all time. I do know that I can't, and don't wish to, forget Aaliya.<br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/deed.en_US" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons License" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380148217i/680962.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>This work is licensed under a <a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/deed.en_US" rel="nofollow noopener">Creative Commons Attribution-NonCommercial-ShareAlike 3.0 Unported License</a>.</span>

<div id="review_1130552659" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1130552659" />
    <a title="Seemita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34622220-seemita"><img alt="Seemita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473185997p2/34622220.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1130552659?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Seemita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Seemita" href="/user/show/34622220-seemita">Seemita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=other-awards-w">other-awards-w</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=singing_words">singing_words</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=me">me</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=america">america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=monologue">monologue</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=nostalgia">nostalgia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=jordan">jordan</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1130552659" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11895468501355423600">Was it necessary to read <b>‘An Unnecessary Woman’?</b> About a woman in the twilight of her life, a product of rusted times? A woman from a foreign land, and of foreign blood? A woman who offered pursed whimpers amid teeth that reeked soupy yellow? One with a musty room and a flickering temper? A borderline linguist who made peace with the unspoken word? She was nothing more than a drifting sprinkle of dust in this swirling world of men and ambition. <br/><br/>May be, it wasn’t. It wasn’t necessary at all to re</span>
  <span id="freeText11895468501355423600" style="display:none">Was it necessary to read <b>‘An Unnecessary Woman’?</b> About a woman in the twilight of her life, a product of rusted times? A woman from a foreign land, and of foreign blood? A woman who offered pursed whimpers amid teeth that reeked soupy yellow? One with a musty room and a flickering temper? A borderline linguist who made peace with the unspoken word? She was nothing more than a drifting sprinkle of dust in this swirling world of men and ambition. <br /><br />May be, it wasn’t. It wasn’t necessary at all to read <b>An Unnecessary Woman</b>. But I read it. And I read her. And read her more. Every page. Every day. Multiple times. In mornings and evenings. In transit and while stationed. Under dim lights. Beneath cloudy skies. I read her. And met her. And met myself. And loved her. And loved myself. Was she I? Not really. She, a septuagenarian and I, almost four decades late. She, a prisoner of Beiruti conservatism and I, a falcon of Indian liberalism. She, a broken rhapsody of relationships and I, a supple bounty of companionship.<br /><br />And yet, she appeared familiar. Almost like the reflection in a broken mirror which makes up for the lost pieces by producing the remnant image, in sparkling, contrasting sharpness. She, Aaliya, was that remnant image. There, within the periphery of what was visible, she cloned me. She, a reluctant neighbor and I, an enthusiastic hermit. She, with a saber tongue and I, with an acerbic voice. She, with her back to the world and I, behind dark-black glasses. She, a passionate translator of Hamsun and Borges, Pessoa and Proust and I, a passionate reader of these great creators. <i>
  <blockquote>Crates, crates, boxes, and crates. The translated manuscripts have the two books, French and English, affixed to the side of the box for identification. Tolstoy, Gogol, and Hamsun; Calvino, Borges, Schulz, Nádas, Nooteboom; Kiš, Karasu, and Kafka; books of memory, disquiet, but not of laughter and forgetting.</blockquote>
</i> Aaliya parades on lyrical comprehensions and deadpan jibes. She is fatally struck by the indifference settled on the eyes of the current generation towards the power of books. She chokes at the comatose sentiment emanating from most streets of the post-war Beirut – the thuds of inertia and acceptance that grinds the air in most homes of her beloved land. And I, well, don’t feel far removed. When random strangers in trains and uninvited visitors at home eye my book or the unruly stack of it with apparent incredulity (and gravely visible shock at times, not to mention the spurt of useless blah-blah aimed at me subsequently), I am swamped by the disdain that Aaliya writes with such flourish in her journal. When poisonous bugs of conservatism, fanaticism, prejudice and stereotypes keep eating at the foundation of the shining body of a nation we are proudly building, I give a muffled cry at the workmen not reading enough to squash them with permanent antidote. Aaliya and I are citizens of the reading world and would shiver if asked to step out of it unless the external world imbibed the ingredients of our present world. <blockquote>
  <i>Literature gives me life, and life kills me.</i>
</blockquote> Was it necessary, then, to read <b>‘An Unnecessary Woman’</b>? Yes. Indeed. Because our antidote is literature, and our dawn, possible.<br /><br />----<br /><br /><i>
  <a href="https://fleetingbrook.wordpress.com/2017/10/10/book-review-an-unnecessary-woman-by-rabih-alameddine-2014/" rel="nofollow noopener">Also on my blog.</a>
</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="11895468501355423600" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_859576522" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/859576522" />
    <a title="Fionnuala" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala"><img alt="Fionnuala" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368182051p2/5498525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/859576522?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fionnuala" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fionnuala" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala">Fionnuala</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=one-book-leads-to-another">one-book-leads-to-another</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=21st-century-classics">21st-century-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer859576522" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1032763381388766496"><br/>The words ‘in my element’ flashed through my mind several times as I was reading this book because I was literally in my element, as if the adverb ‘literally’ and the phrase ‘in my element’ had been invented so that I could apply them to the experience of reading <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18114449.An_Unnecessary_Woman' title='An Unnecessary Woman by Rabih Alameddine' rel='nofollow noopener'>An Unnecessary Woman</a>. It seemed as if the book concerned me and my life in a very personal way, though I am not a self-taught Lebanese intellectual, as is Rabih Alameddine’s narrator, Aaliya Sohbi. <br/><br/>If the book spoke to me so intimately,</span>
  <span id="freeText1032763381388766496" style="display:none"><br />The words ‘in my element’ flashed through my mind several times as I was reading this book because I was literally in my element, as if the adverb ‘literally’ and the phrase ‘in my element’ had been invented so that I could apply them to the experience of reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18114449.An_Unnecessary_Woman" title="An Unnecessary Woman by Rabih Alameddine" rel="nofollow noopener">An Unnecessary Woman</a>. It seemed as if the book concerned me and my life in a very personal way, though I am not a self-taught Lebanese intellectual, as is Rabih Alameddine’s narrator, Aaliya Sohbi. <br /><br />If the book spoke to me so intimately, and surely to many like me, it is because it is about indulging in books the way other people indulge in fine cigars, or chocolate, or spa treatments. It is about being immersed in literature to such an extent that literary associations and connections become part of the way a person operates in the world, every event in their lives recalling a quote or a character or a dilemma in literature. <br /><br />Aaliya Sohbi is someone who believes, as I do, that we appreciate writers better when we copy out their words; I have a notebook full of quotes which I very much enjoyed transcribing in black ink onto fresh white pages. Today I may have shelved the notebook and the ink pen but I still enjoy typing interesting sentences into the updates feature here on goodreads. I always feel I’ve understood a writer’s thoughts better when I’ve taken the time to copy out some of her words, and the thoughts behind the words become more memorable as a result. The reviews I post are a further effort to get under the skin of a reading experience. Some people might call that a waste of time, an 'unnecessary' activity. Such reviews aren’t read by anyone, they might say, or at the very best, they have the life of a mayfly, a brief twenty-four hours in the goodreads sun. What is the point of all that effort for twenty-four brief hours, they ask? I answer that the writing of the reviews has sealed the books into my psyche forever, but they don’t understand such an incorporeal notion or see any utility in it whatsoever.<br /> <br />They would no doubt be even more categorically negative about the ‘unnecessaryness' of the pastime Alameddine has given his narrator: Aaliya translates books into Arabic, but only books that she has read in translation, i.e. books that have been translated from languages she doesn't speak into French and English, the two languages she does speak, along with Arabic. She translates translations as a challenge to herself, and in an effort to understand the translated books better. The translation of translations is entirely for her own pleasure and she never seeks to have them published (a publisher once approached her requesting a translation into Arabic of an American celebrity’s biography but she declined). She takes a year with each translation, working from both the English and the French editions of the book, and when she has finished, she places the hand-written sheets of paper, covered in beautiful Arabic script, carefully in a box which she seals. On the lid, as a kind of inscription, she affixes the French and English versions she has used to make her Arabic conversion. Then she places the box beside the pile of other such boxes in an unused room, a room that resembles a tomb in many ways, since it has no window and no one ever enters it, apart from herself, and then only once a year when she adds a new box. She has never opened the lid of any box once it has been sealed. <br /><br />I should say here that what I have described is revealed in the blurb of the book or in the early pages, and the story Alameddine gives us is only indirectly about Aaliya's translations. She doesn’t do any ‘translating’ inside the pages of this book; she's between translations, as it were, and the account of her Bloom-like walk through the city of Beirut during a three day period at the end of December is very interesting, especially for her literature-filled thoughts while rambling the streets, or while sipping tea in her apartment, or even while trying to avoid talking to her neighbors or her family. But those aspects of the book, while very cleverly done, and a joy to read for the literary, philosophical and classical allusions, are not what I want to focus on. <br /><br />If I’ve stressed the translation aspect of Aaliya’s life, it's because it allows me to examine that provoking word 'unnecessary' in the title. There are many themes in this book that I could focus on: history and geography, philosophy and politics, literature and translation, family and friendship, birth and death, but I only want to deal with that one word, 'unnecessary'. To me, that word is like an electrified probe, it goads me, and I think it goaded Alameddine too, forcing him to write this book around the idea of it. He is making a lot of points with that one word, and he draws on the writings and experiences of several authors to make his case, in particular Bruno Schulz. <br />Schulz, a Polish writer and artist, was shot in the ghetto at Drohobych, having initially been reprieved by an SS officer and labelled a ‘necessary’ Jew (unlike most of the population who, being ‘unnecessary’ were sent to concentration camps or were shot immediately). Schulz's temporary reprieve was ironically due to his artistic abilities; the officer simply needed him to paint murals in his house. Alameddine, by highlighting that anecdote, raises all sorts of questions about the label ‘unnecessary’; in the eyes of Beiruti society (and many other societies), a woman like Aaliya whose parents were Palestinian refugees in Lebanon, who is a divorced (read 'discarded' by her husband), childless bluestocking (and in addition, an unbeliever), a woman who neither knits garments for her relatives, nor eternally chops vegetables for their endless meals, is almost the epitome of ‘unnecessary’.<br /><br />The notion of 'unecessary' can also be applied to another writer referenced frequently in this book (the author mentions over one hundred writers in all, and quotes quite a few of them). The Portuguese writer and poet Fernando Pessoa was dismissed as a dandy in his lifetime and when he died he left a trunkful of unfinished and unpublished manuscripts. His philosophy, <i>the only attitude worthy of a superior man is to persist in an activity he recognizes is useless, to observe a discipline he knows is sterile, and to apply certain norms of philosophical and metaphysical thought that he considers utterly inconsequential</i>, fits with Aaliya’s ‘unnecessary’ mode of existence almost perfectly. Like Pessoa, she understands that her life’s work is not of benefit to anyone but herself yet she is absolutely rigorous in all matters relating to that work, keeping to a strict schedule once she has selected a text to translate and working at it as if her life depended on it. And her life does depend on it, because the making of the translation is everything to her whereas the end result, though not quite nothing, is much less important. <br /><br />I’m reminded of a Czech author whose work is very apropos of this notion of the ‘unnecessary’. Bohumil Hrabal created a character in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/87280.Too_Loud_a_Solitude" title="Too Loud a Solitude by Bohumil Hrabal" rel="nofollow noopener">Too Loud a Solitude</a> whose life was dedicated to the fabrication of art objects, which, though ephemeral, gave a last triumphant moment of glory to the piles of banned books destined for destruction by the Prague authorities. For Hrabal’s hero Hantá, it is also only the process that counts; the product is destroyed on completion (and I wonder if I could have recalled the details of Hrabal’s book if I hadn’t written a review about it, a review which now lies on my gr shelves and may never see the light of a gr day again, but it doesn’t matter, <i>non curo</i>, because the essence of the book will stay in my mind forever).<br />Virginia Woolf said something in her diaries which echoes this idea of process versus result: <i>The truth is, that writing is the profound pleasure and being read the superficial.</i> (and, again, could I have recalled that line had I not used it in my review of her <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14948.A_Writer_s_Diary" title="A Writer's Diary by Virginia Woolf" rel="nofollow noopener">Writer's Diary</a>? I suspect not). <br /><br />Many writers are therefore giving us the same message: that the exercise of writing is what matters the most. And with that thought, it’s time for me to bring this 'unnecessary' exercise to a close - though I’m not too concerned about the conclusion (as I wasn’t very concerned with the ending of the book) because the examination of the thoughts triggered by Alemeddine’s title and by many of his words have satisfied me enough, and more than</span>
  <a data-text-id="1032763381388766496" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7915003-garima?shelf=war-with-peace">war-with-peace</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7915003-garima?shelf=sing-a-song">sing-a-song</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7915003-garima?shelf=my-2-cents">my-2-cents</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7915003-garima?shelf=funny-funsome-sarcasome">funny-funsome-sarcasome</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7915003-garima?shelf=to-re-read">to-re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7915003-garima?shelf=its-not-you-its-meta-or-gfhrytyt">its-not-you-its-meta-or-gfhrytyt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7915003-garima?shelf=world-next-door">world-next-door</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1007441919" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3481661867455678573">The usual mood that prevails while reading ‘An Unnecessary Woman’ is something that can be observed during the time of a candid conversation with a fellow book lover who not only share your passion for books but also have similar reading preferences for most of the part. Mention of a personal favorite writer here, an interesting anecdote there and embellishing such dreamlike atmosphere with some lovely quotes. It’s like a sensible pampering of a reader’s soul in the most fun and exciting way pos</span>
  <span id="freeText3481661867455678573" style="display:none">The usual mood that prevails while reading ‘An Unnecessary Woman’ is something that can be observed during the time of a candid conversation with a fellow book lover who not only share your passion for books but also have similar reading preferences for most of the part. Mention of a personal favorite writer here, an interesting anecdote there and embellishing such dreamlike atmosphere with some lovely quotes. It’s like a sensible pampering of a reader’s soul in the most fun and exciting way possible. <br /><br /><i>I’m not sure that the discovery of love is necessarily more exquisite than the discovery of poetry, or more sensuous for that matter.</i><br /><br />Under the perforated shade of Lebanese skyline, the windows of crumbling apartments are still dusty because of the nature’s fury and human bestiality but Aaliya needs to go on with her life even when the life itself seems indifferent to her existence. This is all acceptable to our protagonist as long as the disquiet of Pessoa empathizes with her world and Yourcenar’s Hadrian is there to give her a royal company. Her introversion is compensated through the regular wanderings within the pages of classics and contemporary literature alike which features the delightful motley of familiar book jackets on display that one look at them and you’re sucked into a hypnotic spell of unadulterated beauty of words. <blockquote>
  <i> Ah, the deliciousness of discovering a masterwork. My heart begins to lift. I can see myself sitting all day in my chair, immersed in lives, plots, and sentences, intoxicated by words and chimeras, paralyzed by satisfaction and contentment, reading until the deepening twilight, until I can no longer make out the words, until my mind begins to wander, until my aching muscles are no longer able to keep the book aloft. Joy is the anticipation of joy.</i>
</blockquote> But SNAP!<br /><br />In a way, it’s possible to both overestimate and underestimate this book. Somewhere post 200 page mark, when the fantastic first impressions mellowed a bit, a kind of reality check appeared out of nowhere or maybe I failed to notice it before in my giddy excitement. I know, I understand and I love the idea of such books but something seemed compromised here and that something was the narrative voice of Aaliya and subsequently Aaliya’s not that unnecessary life. Rabih took over that narrative and not in a convincing way and I can’t give a convincing explanation as to what exactly I mean by that. Simply put, what started out as clever went on to becoming too obvious and ceased to remain fascinating because of the lack of subtlety which I believe this novel demanded and deserved. So all this bothered me to some extent but weighing it against the wonderful time I got out of my reading especially the laugh-out-loud humor and some evocative writing, the eventual deal turned out to be a worthy one. So a recommended read with few caveats and you’ll probably say something of the following nature after reading it:<br /><br />a) Now I know the brilliance other people vouched for.<br />b) I can’t see what the fuss is all about.<br />c) It’s great to good to uh-oh but good nevertheless. (The ever ambivalent Me)<br />d) Work-in-progress options.<br /><br /><i>I’ll probably be incinerated with my books.</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3481661867455678573" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1465997313" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1465997313" />
    <a title="Vessey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30910845-vessey"><img alt="Vessey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1511166278p2/30910845.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1465997313?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vessey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vessey" href="/user/show/30910845-vessey">Vessey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Vessey by: </span>
      Jeffrey
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30910845-vessey?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1465997313" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer270636706707776053"><br/><b>“There is actually no such thing as atheism. There is no such thing as not worshipping. Everybody worships. The only choice we get is what to worship”</b><br/><br/>David Foster Wallace<br/><br/><b>"Perhaps reading and writing books is one of the last defences human dignity has left, because in the end they remind us of what God once reminded us before He too evaporated in this age of relentless humiliations - that we are more than ourselves; that we have souls."</b><br/><br/>Richard Flanagan<br/><br/>Aaliya does not believe in God (to her he is </span>
  <span id="freeText270636706707776053" style="display:none"><br /><b>“There is actually no such thing as atheism. There is no such thing as not worshipping. Everybody worships. The only choice we get is what to worship”</b><br /><br />David Foster Wallace<br /><br /><b>"Perhaps reading and writing books is one of the last defences human dignity has left, because in the end they remind us of what God once reminded us before He too evaporated in this age of relentless humiliations - that we are more than ourselves; that we have souls."</b><br /><br />Richard Flanagan<br /><br />Aaliya does not believe in God (to her he is a Nazi) and the Bible.<br /><br /><i>"Would Noah have allowed a lesbian zebra aboard, an unmarried hedgehog, a limping lemur? Me thinks not.”</i> (Me thinks yes. At least the unmarried hedgehog. <i>Hedgehog</i> sounds kind of aritstocratic.)<br /><br />She worships and believes in literature. Just like God, it has many faces and names, but unlike God, it offers answers. We might happen to like or dislike those answers, but they are always there. We only need to look for them. Of course, the tricky part is to know which ones to look for. Sometimes finding out the right questions to ask is as hard as discovering their answers. What did I ask Aaliya entering her solitary, but rich life? What answers did she give me?<br /><br /><b>Vessey</b>: If you could trade places with any character, who would that be?<br /><br /><b>Aaliya</b>: The quiet one. For he/she is never unprepared. It is those, who know how to listen that know how to speak. I have little to say, but a lot to learn and a lot to give. I have no voice. I have no face. I am as quiet and invisible as a drop of rain piercing boisterous and stormy sea. Yet, I live and speak through others. When my mind crosses with Her mind, I am as sonorous and strong and beautiful as She is. <br /><br /><b>Vessey</b>: And is She cruel or merciful?<br /><br /><b>Aaliya</b>: She is a mirror and we see in Her that which we already have inside of us. She treats as we treat Her. <br /><br /><b>Literature</b>: I am everywhere, I have many names, I have myriad faces, I have millions of friends. And enemies. I have borne so many wounds. I have been mocked, insulted, ignored, left, burned. Many have tried to destroy me. But I always come back. I am ancient and eternal. I know no limits, no death. I possess many worlds, many people. I wield all languages. I am beautiful and complex. I am pleasure and pain, happiness and sorrow, fear and desire. I am you. You will always find yourself in me. I am the mirror of the world. I always take a piece of you and give another in return. What you do with it depends solely on you. My faces are many and different. I have many masters and I am entirely in their power. They all shape me differently. Sometimes I am sublime, sometimes mediocre. Sometimes my voice has vigorous power, sometimes it is barely audible. I unite and divide, I reveal and conceal. I break hearts. I put them back together. I am mystery and revelation. I am Goddess that needs no fear, no submission. I don’t punish, I don’t avenge. I need no sanctuaries, no cults or prayers, no wars in my name. I don’t need to be explained and understood. I need no protection. For I shall always return to those who seek me. I am always reborn. Through you I live many lives. Through me you live many more. We live within each other. Just like Aaliya, all I need is to be needed.<br /><br /><b>Vessey</b>: But what about those heathens that need you not? Do they fill your heart with longing, loneliness? Do you have within you empty spaces, each bearing the shape of some of them?<br /><br /><b>Literature</b>: They rarely seek me. But I always seek <i>them</i>. And it always happens so that some of them find themselves converted. Just like sometimes some of my followers turn against me. They hide away, for sometimes even I cannot help them. My empty spaces have <i>their</i> shapes. For just like I always return, so do they. For I am protection and sanctuary. I am love and addiction. I am forever.<br /><br /><b>Did our short conversation leave me richer, wiser, more ready to listen (to those around me and to <i>myself</i>)? Was my patience justified? Is what I got more than what I gave? And isn’t it an equal exchange (giving and receiving in equal degrees) that makes every journey worthwhile? Isn’t giving just another form of getting and vice versa? I know that amalgamation of the two is in the heart of every profound connection. I was given a lot by Aaliya and I’m giving it all to you, and doing so makes me feel blessed. We live to share and we share to live. I wish to share all that I am with you.</b><br /><br />Read count: 1</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer866676732" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14202593935743809959">I read another review here on Goodreads, and the reviewer stated that he loved Aaliyah. I can do him one better than that: I actually AM Aailyah.<br/><br/>She is a 72 year old, divorced, childless, Beiruti woman, living alone in a decaying apartment, practically abandoned by her mother and half-brothers. I am a little younger, American, married, have a grown daughter, a job, and a fairly active life with friends and family. Outwardly different, but inside my head, just like Aaliya. Exactly the same.<br/><br/>Aaliya</span>
  <span id="freeText14202593935743809959" style="display:none">I read another review here on Goodreads, and the reviewer stated that he loved Aaliyah. I can do him one better than that: I actually AM Aailyah.<br /><br />She is a 72 year old, divorced, childless, Beiruti woman, living alone in a decaying apartment, practically abandoned by her mother and half-brothers. I am a little younger, American, married, have a grown daughter, a job, and a fairly active life with friends and family. Outwardly different, but inside my head, just like Aaliya. Exactly the same.<br /><br />Aaliya worked at a bookstore before she retired, and justified the books she stole, because, well, someone had to read them, didn't they? For 50 years she has also translated her favorites into Arabic from French and English, and carefully hidden them away. Reading has kept her sane. Fictional characters and her favorite authors have been her constant companions.<br /><br />"I long ago abandoned myself to a blind lust for the written word. Literature is my sandbox. In it I play, build my forts and castles, spend glorious time. It is the world outside that box that gives me trouble. I have adapted tamely, though not conventionally, to this visible world so I can retreat without much inconvenience into my inner world of books."<br /><br />Most of this novel takes place inside Aaliyah's mind, which is a lovely, informative place to be if you also love books and music. But toward the end of the novel, the outside world quite literally comes flooding in, and Aaliyah must accept help from her 3 women neighbors, the weird sisters, as she thinks of them. I loved them too, for the way they helped her, and the things we come to know about them. Sometimes we need real people instead of invented ones.<br /><br />I know a lot of people, male and female, many of them Goodreads friends, who are also Aaliyah. If you find yourself smiling and nodding when you read the quote above, then you are Aaliyah too. Exactly the same.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14202593935743809959" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
